    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fccffc81adb544c7a4743e67be0df085">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ba90a2ab-0d58-4b0d-b88d-82a12a828610'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d50888559" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Vanadium-based polyanionic materials are promising electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their outstanding advantages such as high voltage, acceptable specific capacity, excellent structural reversibility, good thermal stability, etc. Polyanionic compounds, moreover, can exhibit excellent multiplicity performance as well as good cycling stability after well-designed carbon covering and bulk-phase doping and thus have attracted the attention of multiple researchers in recent years. In this paper, after the modification of carbon capping and bulk-phase nitrogen doping, compared to pristine Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, the well optimized Na<sub>3</sub>V(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N/C possesses improved electromagnetic induction strength and structural stability, therefore exhibits exceptional cycling capability of 96.11% after 500 cycles at 2&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;80&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) with an elevated voltage platform of 4&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Meanwhile, the designed Na<sub>3</sub>V(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N/C possesses an exceptionally low volume change of ≈0.12% during cycling, demonstrating its quasi-zero strain property, ensuring an impressive capacity retention of 70.26% after 10,000 cycles at 2&nbsp;C. This work provides a facial and cost-effective synthesis method to obtain stable vanadium-based phosphate materials and highlights the enhanced electrochemical properties through the strategy of carbon rapping and bulk-phase nitrogen doping.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202301742-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202301742-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the past few decades, Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been playing an indispensable role in the energy storage field due to their excellent performance, such as high energy density and long life-span performance. LIBs have been applied extensively in mobile devices, electric vehicles, and other energy-storage fields.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0001_R_d50888548e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">1</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0003_R_d50888548e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, with the rapid development of LIBs, the progress of LIBs is retarded by both the scarcity and volatile prices of lithium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0004_R_d50888548e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To meet the growing demand for electrical energy storage technology of the rapidly developed renewable energy sources, many other types of batteries have been developed, such as sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), zinc-ion batteries (ZIBs), aluminum-ion batteries (AIBs), and calcium-ion batteries (CIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0005_R_d50888548e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0010_R_d50888548e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - eScience">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs are considered an up-and-coming energy storage technology because of the similar de-embedding principles to LIBs, the low price of sodium resources, and excellent cycle stability compared to some Li counterparts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0011_R_d50888548e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Battery Energy">11</a>, <a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0012_R_d50888548e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Battery Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, compared to lithium-ion batteries, SIBs are still in the preliminary stages of industrial development. To take full advantage of superior properties in SIBs, it is necessary to further explore more advanced cathode materials with high potential platforms, high capacity, fast charging ability, and good structural stability, which are the key factors for the compositable energy and volume density compared to LiFePO<sub>4</sub> that SIBs should be achieved in the next 5 or 10 years.</p>
         
         <p>Many researchers have focused on three categories of cathode materials for SIBs, such as layered oxide compounds, Prussian blue compounds (PBAs), and polyanionic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0013_R_d50888548e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">13</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0015_R_d50888548e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both layered oxide compounds and PBAs have attracted intensive investigations in the last decades, however, some key scientific issues such as sophisticated phase variations and intrinsic crystal water problems remain. Among these cathode materials, the polyanionic-type compounds, majorly possess the characteristic of 3D sodium-ion de-/insertions channels, which is conducive to accelerating sodium diffusion kinetics and electrochemical performance amelioration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0016_R_d50888548e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Rev.">16</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0018_R_d50888548e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the various polyanionic-type cathode materials including Na<sub>7</sub>V<sub>3</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, NaFePO<sub>4</sub>F, NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>, NaVPO<sub>4</sub>F, etc., Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> has attracted great research attention because of its high specific capacity and NASICON-structure with 3D Na-ion de/insertions frameworks, as well as good thermal stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0019_R_d50888548e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - eScience">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the low potential platform of ≈3.4&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na is a major obstacle that limits its practical application in targeting higher energy density. To address this problem, a well-designed strategy of N<sup>3−</sup> polyanion group doping is proposed to bring about a strong inductive effect, which has been proven to work effectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0020_R_d50888548e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0022_R_d50888548e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The substitution of N<sup>3−</sup> at the central position of tri-tetrahedral can lead to some interesting physical and chemical characterizations since the Fermi level is alternated to higher energy density of state, and it is worth conducting a close investigation toward its extend cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>Therefore, recently newly discovered bulk-N doped carbon modified Na<sub>3</sub>V(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N (NVPN) compounds have been successfully synthesized and attract great attention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0019_R_d50888548e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - eScience">19</a>, <a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0023_R_d50888548e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Mater.">23</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0025_R_d50888548e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Kang's group first reported that NVPN exhibited a structure that three (PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N polyanionic groups centered on one VO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, which showed a high ratio of V:P&nbsp;=&nbsp;1:3.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0023_R_d50888548e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They also proposed that the blocking effect from (PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N tetrahedral is the key factor for maintaining subtle volume change. Chou's group successfully synthesized Na<sub>3</sub>V(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N wrapped with well-designed nitrogen-doped graphene oxide (NGO), which exhibited a redox high potential of 4&nbsp;V and provided a rate performance of 78.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C and 59.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0024_R_d50888548e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both their work also highlight the well-connected 3D sodium diffusion pathways with relatively low energy barriers, therefore the high-rate performance can be reasonably anticipated. In order to improve the stability and rate capability of NVPN, more efforts should be focused on the intrinsic reaction kinetics research for archiving superior electrochemical performance, and the long-term cycling performance of this promising cathode material should be well determined as well.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we synthesized the nanoscale carbon-coated NVPN/C by the sol–gel method. The as-obtained NVPN/C did not require complex synthesizing conditions, which is important for mass production toward industrial application. The carbon-coated NVPN/C shows a clear 4&nbsp;V class redox potential and a high capacity (69.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C, 1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;80&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and a remarkable rate capability of 70.26% retention at 2&nbsp;C after staggering 10,000 cycles. Besides, the kinetics parameters as well as the key reasons behind the outstanding cycling stabilities have been explored, and only imperceptible volume change is clearly determined. In addition, we observe that the Coulombic efficiency of both the initial cycle and subsequent cycles has been improved as well, and it is important to the second sodium extraction toward higher specific capacity. Our work shed light upon the high potentials of this bulk-N doping polyanionic compounds as the long-term cycling cathodes for SIBs, and we believe that this strategy is also feasible for other types of V-based and Fe-based materials that suffer from low working plateaus and unsatisfactory cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202301742-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202301742-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>For the typical synthesizing routes, detailed material synthesis methods are provided in the experimental section in Supporting Information. We first determined its phase characters. The Rietveld refinement analysis is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0001">1a</a>. The high purity as well as high crystallinity are clearly observed, and structurally characterized peaks at 13.23°, 16.21°, 21.02°, 23.04°, 29.92°, and 35.53° with low half-peak width are obviously presented, which correspond to (110), (111), (201), (211), (301), and (312) reflections of NVPN/C, respectively. The Rietveld refinement result with a low <i>R</i>-weighted pattern is obtained from FullProf (R<sub>wp</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6.5%). The final lattice parameters are: <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = <i>c</i> = 9.4374 Å, α = β = γ = 90°, <i>V</i> = 840.55 Å<sup>3</sup>. Remanent refinement results are provided in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The lattice parameter of detected NVPN/C can match well with the cubic symmetry within the space group <i>P2<sub>1</sub>3</i>, which is an unexpected result for this bulk-N doping material since most of the polyanionic compounds possess low space symmetry. Higher symmetry means more dispersed and continuous electronic states, which improve the electronic conductivity and charge transfer efficiency of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0026_R_d50888548e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For a detailed observation, per unit cell of NVPN consists of (PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N Polyanionic groups and VO<sub>6</sub> octahedron that three (PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N are integrated via N atoms, and one VO<sub>6</sub> octahedron is shared with three (PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N. This sharing of oxygen atoms causes a strong inductive effect on the transition metal, which increases the redox potential of element V. The crystal structural illustration is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0001">1b</a>. It can be seen that there are three different sites of Na, and the second Na<sup>+</sup> extraction is supposed to have happened at Na (2) site since it has no direct connections to the adjacent VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. To further determine the molecular structure and valence states of the materials, the samples were further analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) shows the full XPS spectrum and detailed peak information of the elements associated with the chemical formulas of Na, V, P, O, N, and C. The purity of the synthesized samples is further verified by the results that the full spectrum contains only Na, V, P, O, N, and C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0001">1c</a>, V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> located at respectively 523.52 and 516.33&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating the trivalent state of V compared to previous results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0027_R_d50888548e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The N 1s spectrum (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0002">2d</a>) clearly demonstrates the presence of nitrogen atoms with Pyrrolic N and Graphitic N located at 400.35 and 398.55&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0028_R_d50888548e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a>, <a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0029_R_d50888548e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Nano">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These two peaks of N also indicate that the bulk N in the lattice also shows similar physical characters with the N atoms in the carbon coating layer. Furthermore, according to the Fourier Transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), several peaks characterized by V─O vibrations distribute in the low wavelength region (500–1000&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0030_R_d50888548e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0031_R_d50888548e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Solid State Sci.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the bending/stretching vibrations ≈1000&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> arise from the VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and PO<sub>3</sub>N tetrahedra of the NVPN/C material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0024_R_d50888548e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the thermogravimetric data, the C content in NVPN/C is calculated to 2.21% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0001">1e</a>), which is an appropriate amount for industrial utilization. The Raman spectrum was performed to further understand the degree of graphitization of the sample. The two characteristic peaks of carbon atoms can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0001">1f</a>, which are D-band and G-band located at 1346.6 and 1593.4&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. The D band indicates carbon defects in the crystal lattice, and the G band, caused by in-plane stretching vibrations in the sp2 orbitals of C atoms, is used to characterize the degree of carbonization of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0032_R_d50888548e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Nano">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By fitting of observed curve, the value of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is calculated to be 1.03, which indicates a high degree of graphitization and good electrical conductivity of the as-prepared material. Several bands near 1000&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are consistent with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> samples, and these bands can be attributed to the stretching vibrations of PO<sub>3</sub>N.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0033_R_d50888548e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the adsorption and desorption curves of NVPN/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0001">1g</a>), it can be found that in the low-pressure region, the interactions between adsorbent molecules are greater than the interactions between the adsorbent and the adsorbate. Thus, the adsorbent is initially more difficult to adsorb, and the adsorption accelerates markedly with the increase of the pressure, and multilayers of adsorption also occur. The formation of hysteresis loop by the desorption process indicates the formation of homogeneous mesopores in the material with a pore size of 2–8&nbsp;nm, which is the key factor that favors the cyclic stability of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0034_R_d50888548e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0035_R_d50888548e812" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Carbon">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NVPN/C possesses a porous structure and a specific surface area of 8.5118&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which facilitates the accumulation of surface-activated molecules and enhances the diffusion rate of ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0036_R_d50888548e822" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301742-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/32daa536-2d87-48a4-a719-07323d140a2f/smtd202301742-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/32daa536-2d87-48a4-a719-07323d140a2f/smtd202301742-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e36c339e-8e92-4a5f-9a7e-e525d00c43b4/smtd202301742-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/32daa536-2d87-48a4-a719-07323d140a2f/smtd202301742-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301742-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301742" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a)Rietveld refinement of NVPN/C; b) Schematic crystal structure of NVPN/C, the V(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N group, the VO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and (PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N group. XPS spectra of c) V 2<i>p</i>, d)N 1<i>s</i>. e) Raman spectra of NVPN/C. f) Thermogravimetric curves of NVPN/C. g) Adsorption–desorption curves and pore size scatter plots(inset) of NVPN/C under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301742-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0adb2775-1d69-4865-9a98-27c2bc1a4024/smtd202301742-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0adb2775-1d69-4865-9a98-27c2bc1a4024/smtd202301742-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/45cf7e44-619a-4b21-932c-dfa8528e5a8d/smtd202301742-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0adb2775-1d69-4865-9a98-27c2bc1a4024/smtd202301742-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301742-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301742" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) TEM images. c) HRTEM image. d) Selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of NVPN/C. e) SEM-energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) mapping images of NVPN/C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Scanning electron microscope (SEM) images and the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0002">2a,b</a>. The overall structure of the NVPN/C can be observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), which displays a sheet-like morphology. The (111) lattice plane was confirmed by measuring the lattice spacing by comparing the standard lattice plane parameters. As for the cubic structure, the (111) reflection is important because the major characters of unique performance arise from this plane (within the body-centered atom). Carbon boundaries and distinct lattice streaks at the edges of the particles can be clearly observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0002">2c</a>, indicating the well-constructed carbon coating layer during the distinct synthesizing route. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0002">2d</a> presents the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern showing that the material has a good spatial structure within the space group <i>P2<sub>1</sub>3</i>, with the calculated zone aix of [1–10]. The homogeneous distribution of each element in NVPN/C could be clearly observed in Energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0002">2e</a>), indicating that nitrogen is uniformly doped into the materials and the materials are successfully synthesized as expected.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of the NVPN/C sample was tested by assembling half-cell with sodium metal as the negative electrode. The applied electrolyte is 1 м NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (EC/PC,1:1 by volume) with 5&nbsp;vol% fluoroethylene carbonates (FEC). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the charge/discharge curves of NVPN/C at 0.2&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;80&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) between 3 and 4.25&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). These curves clearly show charge and discharge plateau ≈4&nbsp;V, corresponding to V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> redox.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0037_R_d50888548e929" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Electrochem. Commun.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further exploration is mainly focusing on the continuous oxidation of V<sup>4+</sup> to V<sup>5+</sup> for higher specific capacity. The material is tested for rate properties in the range of 0.1 and 10&nbsp;C, shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3b</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3c</a> shows the corresponding charge/discharge curves. At 0.1, 0.5, 1, 5, and 10&nbsp;C, the electrode delivers a discharge capacity of 69.5, 61.2, 57.8, 48.8, and 36.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Remarkably, when the rate turns back to 0.1 from 10&nbsp;C, the discharge capacity recovers to 69.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating outstanding superior rate capability as well as reversibility of the electrode. Such high-rate performance is mainly attributed to the 3D sodium ion diffusion pathways and the well-constructed carbon network. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3d</a>. Two typical redox potentials of this material at ≈3.42/3.24&nbsp;V, and 4.13/3.98&nbsp;V, corresponding to the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup> redox of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and NVPN/C, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0038_R_d50888548e966" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nanoscale">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth to mention that we have attempted various methods to eliminate the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> during synthesizing procedures, however, the bulk-N cannot substitute all of the centered O atoms even in a pure NH<sub>3</sub> atmosphere. The weak polarization of the current-voltage curves from the first to the third cycle at a sweep rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> proves that the material possesses excellent reversibility, and the almost overlapping current-voltage curves for multiple times with the same sweep rate also proves the stability of the electrochemical properties of the material. Besides, the sample has outstanding capacity retention in long-cycle performance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3e</a> demonstrates a high initial specific capacity of 69.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;C and a satisfactory capacity retention of 94.26% after 500 cycles. Similarly, the NVPN/C shows a high-capacity retention of 96.11% under 2&nbsp;C after 500 cycles, indicating its good stability under high current density.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301742-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b5fa7135-1206-4418-bf9c-0dbf8c244526/smtd202301742-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b5fa7135-1206-4418-bf9c-0dbf8c244526/smtd202301742-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c29bfe49-f169-4c6e-b6dd-3ced06b26812/smtd202301742-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b5fa7135-1206-4418-bf9c-0dbf8c244526/smtd202301742-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301742-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301742" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Charge–discharge curves for the first five cycles of NVPN/C at 0.2&nbsp;C. b) Rate performances of NVPN/C from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;C. c) Charge–discharge curve from 0.1 to 10&nbsp;C. d) Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves for the first three cycles of NVPN/C at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. e) Comparison of cycling performance at different rates (0.2 and 2&nbsp;C). f) Ultralong cycling performances at 2&nbsp;C. g) Comparison of cycling performance and voltage of mainstream cathode materials for various sodium-ion batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to further explore the long-cycle properties of the material, an ultralong cycle performance test with 10,000 cycles was performed, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3f</a>. It is noted that the capacity retention is exceptional, ≈70.26% even after 10,000 cycles at 2&nbsp;C and the coulombic efficiency maintains almost 100% at each cycle, demonstrating the practical application of NVPN/C with excellent cycling stability even only one Na<sup>+</sup> can be extracted up till now. Electrochemical reactivity and electron conductivity can improve cycling stability performance by reducing polarization, increasing rate capability, and preventing electrode material degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0039_R_d50888548e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By comparing with other cathode materials for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3g</a>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0040_R_d50888548e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">40</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0059_R_d50888548e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the capacity loss of NVPN/C per cycle is fairly low and the working voltage is high, this comparison reinforces the N doping in NVPN/C can enhance the electrochemical reactivity and electronic conductivity,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0060_R_d50888548e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Carbon">60</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0062_R_d50888548e1034" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Adv. Mater.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which additionally contributes to excellent cyclic stability. Compared to other high-voltage polyanionic cathodes, the F-substituted polyanionic compounds normally possess a high working voltage platform but suffer from the large volume strain, therefore most of them cannot sustain over 5000 cycles. Then the cells were disassembled after 800 and 10,000 cycles (cycling performance at 0.2&nbsp;C with 800 cycles is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), respectively, and further XRD (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and SEM (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) tests were performed. As observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the intensity of the characteristic peak at 65° was greatly increased, which indicated that the crystals underwent a selective orientational growth. The perforation fracture of electrodes from detached cells can be seen in the scanning electron microscope images (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), which were probably due to the decrease in the strength of the grains themselves after the growth as well as the increase in the internal stresses within the crystals.</p>
         
         <p>In order to further determine the kinetic characters of as-obtained NVPN/C cathode, we employed several approaches to detect the precise status of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion during cycling. Pseudocapacitance contribution and diffusion coefficient of sodium ions (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8edaaf64-70df-4653-8aec-26c48fc95cc0/smtd202301742-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) were calculated to analyze the reaction kinetics of the electrodes. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the CV curves of the test at five different sweep speeds of 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The curves include two redox potentials of NVPN/C at 3.71/3.51&nbsp;V, 4.21/3.93&nbsp;V, respectively, and the lower voltage platform is attributed to the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> impurity material. The current value of the oxidation/reduction peak has the same trend of increasing/decreasing with the increase in sweep speed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4b</a>). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4c</a>, the <i>b</i>-values are calculated to be 0.53, 0.79, 0.75, and 0.69 for the peaks of 1, 2, 3 and 4, respectively. The slope can quantitatively reflect the size of the diffusion coefficient, and the value range is generally between 0.5 and 1. The larger the slope, the larger the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions, and a larger diffusion coefficient determines the battery's ability to discharge at high currents, a testament to the material's potential to work at high currents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0063_R_d50888548e1107" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - ACS Nano">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
            <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e78ddc12-bd76-4021-9579-58579764f15b/smtd202301742-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> during the cell reaction was calculated to understand the kinetic behavior. The results are shown in both Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), with the pseudocapacitance contribution of 47% at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The detailed calculation process is shown in the Supporting Information. Pseudocapacitance is a Faraday process that occurs on the surface of the electrode material and has a clear boundary with capacitance, a non-Faraday process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0064_R_d50888548e1136" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - ACS Nano">64</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0066_R_d50888548e1136" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - J. Phys. Chem. C.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, since the pseudocapacitance reaction is faster than the double-layer capacitance formation at the electrode/electrolyte interface, at higher scanning speeds, the double-layer capacitance formation is limited by the diffusion of ions in the electrolyte, whereas the pseudocapacitance reaction can still take place at the electrode surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0067_R_d50888548e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - RSC Adv.">67</a>-<a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0070_R_d50888548e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the pseudocapacitance ratio increases with scanning speed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4e</a>), which explains why the material has excellent cycling stability from one important aspect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0071_R_d50888548e1150" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - Nanotechnology">71</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Nyquist spectrum measured by the material consists of a semicircle and a straight line (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4f</a>), and the fitted equivalent circuit is shown at the top of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4g</a>. Details of the fitting procedure are given in the Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). The <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> value is the solution resistance, which belongs to the low-frequency region controlled by the material's electrolyte, and its value remains constant, indicating that the electrolyte is relatively stable. Whereas the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value is part of the high-frequency region controlled by charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0072_R_d50888548e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - Adv. Mater.">72</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The fitting results of EIS are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). It is shown that the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> decreases as the cycling going, especially drops sharply from 1399 to 902.7&nbsp;Ω after 10 cycles, and finally became relatively stable at 402.3&nbsp;Ω after 200 cycles. The decrease of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value is related to the gradual formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film, which indicates that the SEI film grows gradually during the cycling process, avoiding the damage of the solvent molecules embedding on the electrode material, and also improving the cycling stability of the material to some degree. <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0057_R_d50888548e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Energy Storage Mater.">57</a>, <a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0073_R_d50888548e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Adv. Mater.">73</a>, <a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0074_R_d50888548e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 74 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">74</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The variation range of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/146c3824-0afa-485f-a042-6f5f1c8f13ac/smtd202301742-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> is 10<sup>−13</sup>–10<sup>−14</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, which is obtained by calculating the inclined straight-line data in the low-frequency region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4g</a>). The results show that the σ of the NVPN/C with 1 cycle is the smallest (46.1), reflecting the largest <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b05f450a-8db6-43e2-8893-3327e6ee0c4a/smtd202301742-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0004.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> value (Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), and as the cycle proceeds, the value of σ gradually stabilized.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0075_R_d50888548e1237" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 75 - J. Phys. Chem. C">75</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d6384353-855c-4737-b047-b844544b47a8/smtd202301742-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0005.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> can also be measured by GITT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4h</a>; Equation <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), and the curve of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d3279560-e080-4774-91be-1080f8e9d28d/smtd202301742-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0006.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> as a function of voltage (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0004">4h</a>) is calculated from the GITT curve of NVPN/C. <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/290643e7-0a73-47b6-9ff9-ab52a0982e85/smtd202301742-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0007.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> shows local minimum at 3.3–3.5&nbsp;V and 3.9–4.1&nbsp;V, corresponding to the two voltage plateaus of NVPN/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0003">3a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0058_R_d50888548e1300" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nano Energy">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NVPN/C undergoes a phase transition from rhombohedral to monoclinic structure during the first sodium extraction, which reduces the sodium diffusion coefficient by two orders of magnitude. All the above-obtained data have&nbsp;proved that the as-prepared possess satisfactory sodium diffusion kinetics at various current densities since the 3D Na<sup>+</sup> transfer channels are extremely important, which is critical for the high demand of power density as well as the energy density of Electrochemical energy storage devices. With the increase of the electrode density, the porosity and surface area of the electrode will be reduced, which is not conducive to the diffusion of sodium ions, and it will increase the polarization internal resistance, but the conductivity will increase and the ohmic internal resistance will decrease, and this parabolic phenomenon is the result of the balance of these two effects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smtd202301742-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smtd202301742-bib-0025_R_d50888548e1308" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301742-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/35d6ef41-1095-4420-822c-885ce5945da5/smtd202301742-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/35d6ef41-1095-4420-822c-885ce5945da5/smtd202301742-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cfbaf742-317a-4748-871f-069b8c56060a/smtd202301742-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/35d6ef41-1095-4420-822c-885ce5945da5/smtd202301742-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301742-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301742" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at different scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Peak current versus square root of scan rate (v<sup>1/2</sup>) fitting curves. c) Logarithmic peak current (log(<i>I</i>, current)) versus logarithmic scan rate (log(<i>v</i>, scan rate))fitting curve. d) pseudo-capacitance (green filled area) and CV curve (orange filled area) at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. e) Percentage bar charts of pseudo-capacitance and diffusion. f) 3D Nyquist spectrum (1, 10, 100, and 200 cycles from back to front). g) Fitted straight line between the reciprocal of the square root of the angular frequency(ω<sup>−1/2</sup>) and real parts of the impedance(Z'), and the fitted equivalent circuit(inset). h) charge and discharge curves of Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) pattern and Logarithmic value of the diffusion coefficient(lg <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4c67803f-d623-4a88-8e28-6b97ca70e760/smtd202301742-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:23669608:media:smtd202301742:smtd202301742-math-0008" display="inline" location="graphic/smtd202301742-math-0008.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <msub>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                    <mo>+</mo>
                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math>) of sodium ions versus voltage(V) curves(inset).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In situ XRD is able to observe changes in crystal structure during the charging and discharging process. The whole in situ XRD spectrum (2<i>θ</i> from 10 to 60°) is placed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that almost all the peaks are identified with high intensities. Here, the variation of XRD peaks at different voltages from 2.8 to 4.3&nbsp;V is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0005">5a,b</a>. None of the peaks appear abnormally shifted and can be well identified with the space group as cubic NVPN/C of <i>P2<sub>1</sub>3</i>, and the reversibility of all the observed planes is outstanding during the testing two cycles. From the major reflection of (111), only subtle changes of 2<i>θ</i> can be observed. The change&nbsp;in volume during charging and discharging was analyzed further (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smtd202301742-fig-0005">5c</a>). The change in the value of cell parameter <i>a</i>-axis is very small and the average value of <i>a</i>-axis is 9.4349&nbsp;Å, which is very close to the pristine value. What's more, the material has a maximum volume change of only 0.12%, verifying the structural stability and quasi-zero-strain properties of NVPN/C. This quasi-zero strain property ensures highly structural stability and cycling reversibility by avoiding structural changes aroused from the expansion and contraction of the material during charging and discharging, that is why the NVPN/C shows great promising for practical application, especially for all-solid-state battery systems since the external pressure only allows very small volume change during extend cycles.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smtd202301742-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d8e0b370-fbfa-4aa1-9a5a-29316294b966/smtd202301742-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d8e0b370-fbfa-4aa1-9a5a-29316294b966/smtd202301742-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/849f98ba-42cb-4a58-a42c-d9642f5ca5a5/smtd202301742-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d8e0b370-fbfa-4aa1-9a5a-29316294b966/smtd202301742-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smtd202301742-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202301742" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Two cycles of charge/discharge electrochemical curves (the left) and 2D color-filled contour image (the right). b) in situ XRD patterns. c) lattice parameters and volume change of NVPN/C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smtd202301742-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smtd202301742-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, the as-synthesized Na<sub>3</sub>V(PO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>N/C materials with a sheet-like 2D structure exhibit a significant enhancement of cycling performance with well-constructed carbon coating layers. Bulk-phase nitrogen doping of the NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> improves the electromagnetic induction strength and enhances the structural stability, resulting in excellent cycling performance (e.g., capacity retention of 96.11% after 500 cycles at 2&nbsp;C). The kinetic investigations correspond well with the high-rate performance since the 3D diffusion pathways are clearly demonstrated. In addition, in situ XRD measurements reveal an extremely low volume change of the NVPN/C during cycling (≈0.12%), demonstrating the material's quasi-zero strain characteristic which ensures the ultra-high-capacity retention (70.26%) of the electrode after 10,000 cycles at 2&nbsp;C. This work provides a facile synthesizing route for mass production and a deeper understanding of bulk-phase nitrogen-doped vanadium-based phosphate material and also demonstrates the potential application of NVPN/C materials in future sodium-ion battery cathode materials. With further studies focusing on the second Na ion extraction, it can be reasonably anticipated that higher energy density and power densities can be well achieved and balanced simultaneously.</p>
         </section>
